<p>The economy, which had been through two recessions in three years, and was in one when Kennedy took office, accelerated notably during his presidency. Despite low <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> and interest rates, <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> had grown by an average of only 2.2% per annum during the Eisenhower presidency (scarcely more than population growth at the time), and had declined by 1% during Eisenhower's last twelve months in office.<sup id="cite_ref-Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> GDP expanded by an average of 5.5% from early 1961 to late 1963,<sup id="cite_ref-Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> inflation remained steady at around 1%,<sup id="cite_ref-GDP_1913_to_2002_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GDP_1913_to_2002-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> and unemployment dropped from nearly 7 percent in January 1961 to 5.5 percent in December 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006127,_141_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006127,_141-162">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Industrial production rose by 15% and motor vehicle sales rose by 40%.<sup id="cite_ref-Statistical_Abstract_1964_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Statistical_Abstract_1964-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> This sustained rate of growth in GDP and industry continued until around 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was the first president to fully endorse <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a>, which emphasized the importance of economic growth as opposed to inflation or deficits.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> He ended a period of tight fiscal policies, loosening monetary policy to keep <a href="/wiki/Interest_rate" title="Interest rate">interest rates</a> down and to encourage growth of the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrum2000293_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrum2000293-166">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> He presided over the first government budget to top the $100&#160;billion mark, in 1962, and his first budget in 1961 led to the country's first non-war, non-recession <a href="/wiki/Government_budget_deficit" class="mw-redirect" title="Government budget deficit">deficit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrum2000324_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrum2000324-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1962, as the economy continued to grow, Kennedy became concerned with the issue of inflation. He asked companies and unions to work together to keep prices low, and met initial success.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrinkley201215–17_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrinkley201215–17-168">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> He implemented guideposts developed by the Council of Economic Advisers that were designed to avoid <a href="/wiki/Price/wage_spiral" title="Price/wage spiral">wage-price spirals</a> in key industries such as steel and automobiles. Kennedy was proud that his Labor Department help keep wages steady in the steel industry, but was outraged in April 1962, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Blough" title="Roger Blough">Roger Blough</a>, the president of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Steel" title="U.S. Steel">U.S. Steel</a>, quietly informed Kennedy that his company would raise prices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParmet1983238_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParmet1983238-169">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In response, Attorney General Robert Kennedy began a price-fixing investigation against U.S. Steel, and Presiddent Kennedy convinced other steel companies to rescind their price increases until finally even U.S. Steel, isolated and in danger of being undersold, agreed to rescind its own price increase.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006130–134_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006130–134-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Aside from his conflict with U.S. Steel, Kennedy generally maintained good relations with corporate leaders compared to his Democratic predecessors Truman and FDR, and his administration did not escalate the enforcement of <a href="/wiki/United_States_antitrust_law" title="United States antitrust law">antitrust law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006135–136_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006135–136-172">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> His administration also implemented new tax policies designed to encourage business investment.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png/280px-Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png/420px-Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png/560px-Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png 2x" data-file-width="1776" data-file-height="1013" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gallup_Poll-Approval_Rating-John_F_Kennedy.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graph of Kennedy's <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> approval ratings</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Walter_Heller" title="Walter Heller">Walter Heller</a>, who served as the chairman of the CEA, advocated for a Keynesian-style tax cut designed to help spur economic growth, and Kennedy adopted this policy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996464–465_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996464–465-174">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The idea was that a tax cut would stimulate consumer demand, which in turn would lead to higher economic growth, lower unemployment, and increased federal revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006125_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006125-175">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> To the disappointment of liberals like <a href="/wiki/John_Kenneth_Galbraith" title="John Kenneth Galbraith">John Kenneth Galbraith</a>, Kennedy's embrace of the tax cut also shifted his administration's focus away from the proposed old-age health insurance program and other domestic expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006136–137_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006136–137-176">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In January 1963, Kennedy presented Congress with a tax cut that would reduce the top marginal tax rate from 91 percent to 65 percent, and lower the corporate tax rate from 52 percent to 47 percent. The predictions according to the Keynesian model indicated the cuts would decrease income taxes by about $10 billion and corporate taxes by about $3.5 billion. The plan also included reforms designed to reduce the impact of <a href="/wiki/Itemized_deduction" title="Itemized deduction">itemized deductions</a>, as well as provisions to help the elderly and handicapped. Republicans and many Southern Democrats opposed the bill, calling for simultaneous reductions in expenditures, but debate continued throughout 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006139–141_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006139–141-177">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> In February 1964, three months Into Johnson's administration, Congress approved the <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1964" title="Revenue Act of 1964">Revenue Act of 1964</a>, which lowered the top individual rate to 70 percent, and the top corporate rate to 48 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_presidency">Early presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Early presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg/170px-Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg/255px-Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg/340px-Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3344" data-file-height="5040" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thurgood_Marshall_1957-09-17.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In May 1961 Kennedy appointed <a href="/wiki/Thurgood_Marshall" title="Thurgood Marshall">Thurgood Marshall</a> to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Second_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit">U.S. Court of Appeals</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The turbulent end of state-sanctioned racial discrimination was one of the most pressing domestic issues of the 1960s. <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" class="mw-redirect" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a> <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">segregation</a> had been established law in the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> for much of the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-Grantham_p156_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grantham_p156-179">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> had ruled in 1954 in <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a></i> that <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> in public schools was unconstitutional. Many schools, especially in southern states, did not obey the Supreme Court's decision. The Supreme Court's prohibition of segregation at public facilities like buses, restaurants, theaters, courtrooms, bathrooms, and beaches was also defied.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2003292–293_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2003292–293-180">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy favored desegregation and other civil rights causes, but he generally did not place a high priority on civil rights, especially before 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996473–475_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996473–475-181">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Recognizing that conservative Southern Democrats could block legislation, Kennedy did not introduce civil rights legislation upon taking office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002487_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002487-182">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy did appoint many blacks to office, including civil rights attorney Thurgood Marshall.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy also established the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission#Background" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Commission">President's Committee on Equal Employment Opportunity</a> to investigate employment discrimination and expanded the Justice Department's involvement in voting rights cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996473–475_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996473–475-181">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy believed the grassroots movement for civil rights would anger many Southern whites and make it more difficult to pass civil rights laws in Congress, and he distanced himself from it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryant200660,_66_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryant200660,_66-184">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> As articulated by brother Robert, the administration's early priority was to "keep the president out of this civil rights mess."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Civil rights movement participants, mainly those on the front line in the South, viewed Kennedy as lukewarm,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> especially concerning the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Riders" title="Freedom Riders">Freedom Riders</a>. The Freedom Riders organized an integrated public transportation effort in the South and were repeatedly met with white mob violence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490_183-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Robert Kennedy, speaking for the president, urged the Freedom Riders to "get off the buses and leave the matter to peaceful settlement in the courts."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993123–126_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993123–126-185">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy feared sending federal troops would stir up "hated memories of <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a>" among conservative Southern whites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490_183-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Displeased with Kennedy's pace addressing the issue of segregation, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> and his associates produced a document in 1962 calling on the president to follow in the footsteps of <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> and use an executive order to deliver a blow for civil rights as a kind of "<a href="/wiki/Second_Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Second Emancipation Proclamation">Second Emancipation Proclamation</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1962, <a href="/wiki/James_Meredith" title="James Meredith">James Meredith</a> enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mississippi" title="University of Mississippi">University of Mississippi</a> but was prevented from entering. Attorney General Robert Kennedy responded by sending 400 federal marshals, while President Kennedy reluctantly sent 3,000 troops after the situation on campus turned violent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryant200671_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryant200671-187">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ole_Miss_riot_of_1962" title="Ole Miss riot of 1962">Ole Miss riot of 1962</a> left two dead and dozens injured, but Meredith did finally enroll in his first class. Kennedy regretted not sending in troops earlier and he began to doubt whether the "evils of Reconstruction" he had been taught or believed were true.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490_183-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer_2002490-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>  On November 20, 1962, Kennedy signed <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_11063" title="Executive Order 11063">Executive Order 11063</a>, prohibiting racial discrimination in federally supported housing or "related facilities".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2003580_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2003580-188">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abolition_of_the_poll_tax">Abolition of the poll tax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Abolition of the poll tax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Poll_tax_history.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Poll_tax_history.gif/240px-Poll_tax_history.gif" decoding="async" width="240" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Poll_tax_history.gif/360px-Poll_tax_history.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Poll_tax_history.gif 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Poll_tax_history.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fea200; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Poll tax</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Cumulative poll tax (missed poll taxes from prior years must also be paid to vote)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7d7f7c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;No poll tax</div> History of the poll tax by state from 1868 to 1966</div></div></div>
<p>Sensitive to criticisms of the administration's commitment to protecting the constitutional rights of minorities at the ballot box, Attorney General Robert Kennedy, early in 1962, urged the president to press Congress to take action. Rather than proposing comprehensive legislation, President Kennedy put his support behind a proposed constitutional amendment that would prohibit states from conditioning the right to vote in <a href="/wiki/US_federal_election" class="mw-redirect" title="US federal election">federal elections</a> on payment of a <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Poll tax (United States)">poll tax</a> or other types of tax. He considered the constitutional amendment the best way to avoid a filibuster, as the claim that federal abolition of the poll tax was unconstitutional would be moot. Still, some liberals opposed Kennedy's action, feeling that an amendment would be too slow compared to legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The poll tax was one of several laws that had been enacted by states across the South to <a href="/wiki/Disenfranchisement_after_the_Reconstruction_Era" title="Disenfranchisement after the Reconstruction Era">disenfranchise and marginalize black citizens from politics</a> so far as practicable without violating the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifteenth Amendment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Several civil rights groups<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> opposed the proposed amendment on the grounds that it "would provide an immutable precedent for shunting all further civil rights legislation to the amendment procedure."<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The amendment was passed by both houses of Congress in August 1962, and sent to the states for <a href="/wiki/Ratification" title="Ratification">ratification</a>. It was ratified on January 23, 1964, by the requisite number of states (38), becoming the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fourth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twenty-fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1963">1963</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 1963">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights,_11_June_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg/210px-President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg/315px-President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg/420px-President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="963" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights,_11_June_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Address" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Rights Address">addressing the nation on civil rights</a>, June 11, 1963</div></div></div>
<p>Disturbed by the violent reaction to the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_campaign" title="Birmingham campaign">civil rights campaign in Birmingham</a>, and eager to prevent further violence or damage to U.S. foreign relations, Kennedy took a more active stance on civil rights in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996480–481_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996480–481-194">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> On June 11, 1963, President Kennedy intervened when Alabama Governor <a href="/wiki/George_Wallace" title="George Wallace">George Wallace</a> blocked the <a href="/wiki/Stand_in_the_Schoolhouse_Door" title="Stand in the Schoolhouse Door">doorway</a> to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a> to stop two African American students, <a href="/wiki/Vivian_Malone" class="mw-redirect" title="Vivian Malone">Vivian Malone</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Hood" title="James Hood">James Hood</a>, from attending. Wallace moved aside only after being confronted by Deputy Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Katzenbach" title="Nicholas Katzenbach">Nicholas Katzenbach</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_National_Guard" title="Alabama National Guard">Alabama National Guard</a>, which had just been federalized by order of the president. That evening Kennedy delivered a major <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Address" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Rights Address">address on civil rights</a> on national television and radio. In it he launched his initiative for civil rights legislation that would guarantee equal access to public schools and other facilities, the equal administration of justice, and also provide greater protection of voting rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993521–523_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993521–523-195">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's embrace of civil rights causes would cost him in the South; Gallup polls taken in September 1963 would show his approval rating at 44 percent in the South, compared to a national approval rating of 62 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006201_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006201-197">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> As the president had predicted, the day after his civil rights address, and in reaction to it, House Majority leader <a href="/wiki/Carl_Albert" title="Carl Albert">Carl Albert</a> called to advise him that his effort to extend the Area Redevelopment Act had been defeated, primarily by the votes of Southern Democrats and Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993524_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993524-198">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28,_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28%2C_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg/210px-Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28%2C_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28%2C_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg/315px-Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28%2C_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28%2C_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg/420px-Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28%2C_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2936" data-file-height="2944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_Meeting_with_Leaders_of_the_March_on_Washington_August_28,_1963_-_NARA_-_194276.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy meets with leaders of the <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="March on Washington">March on Washington</a> in the Oval Office, August 28, 1963.</div></div></div>
<p>A crowd of over one hundred thousand, predominantly African Americans, gathered in Washington for the civil rights <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom</a> on August 28, 1963. Kennedy initially opposed the march, fearing it would have a negative effect on the prospects for the civil rights bills pending in Congress. These fears were heightened just prior to the march when <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> Director <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a> presented the administration with allegations that some of civil rights leader <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a>'s close advisers, specifically <a href="/wiki/Hunter_Pitts_O%27Dell" title="Hunter Pitts O&#39;Dell">Jack O'Dell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Levison" title="Stanley Levison">Stanley Levison</a>, were communists.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> When King ignored the administration's warning, Robert Kennedy issued a directive authorizing the FBI to wiretap King and other leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">Southern Christian Leadership Conference</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Although Kennedy only gave written approval for limited wiretapping of King's phones "on a trial basis, for a month or so",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372-201">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Hoover extended the clearance so his men were "unshackled" to look for evidence in any areas of King's life they deemed worthy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372–374_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372–374-202">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> The wiretapping continued through June 1966 and was revealed in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-the_atlantic_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_atlantic-203">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The task of coordinating the federal government's involvement in the August 28 March on Washington was given to the Department of Justice, which channeled several hundreds thousand dollars to the six sponsors of the March, including the NAACP and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">Southern Christian Leadership Conference</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993580–584_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993580–584-204">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> To ensure a peaceful demonstration, the organizers and the president personally edited speeches that were inflammatory and collaborated on all aspects related to times and venues. Thousands of troops were placed on standby. Kennedy watched King's speech on TV and was very impressed. The March was considered a "triumph of managed protest", and not one arrest relating to the demonstration occurred. Afterwards, the March leaders accepted an invitation to the White House to meet with Kennedy and photos were taken. Kennedy felt that the March was a victory for him as well and bolstered the chances for his civil rights bill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993580–584_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993580–584-204">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Notwithstanding the success of the March, the larger struggle was far from over. Three weeks later, <a href="/wiki/16th_Street_Baptist_Church_bombing" title="16th Street Baptist Church bombing">a bomb exploded</a> on Sunday, September 15 at the 16th Street Baptist Church in Birmingham; by the end of the day, four African American children had died in the explosion, and two other children shot to death in the aftermath.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993599–600_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993599–600-205">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Due to this resurgent violence, the civil rights legislation underwent some drastic amendments that critically endangered any prospects for its passage. An outraged president called congressional leaders to the White House and by the following day the original bill, without the additions, had enough votes to get it out of the House committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993628–631_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993628–631-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Gaining Republican support, Senator <a href="/wiki/Everett_Dirksen" title="Everett Dirksen">Everett Dirksen</a> promised the legislation would be brought to a vote, preventing a <a href="/wiki/Filibuster_in_the_United_States_Senate" title="Filibuster in the United States Senate">Senate filibuster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002492_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002492-207">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The following summer, on July 2, the guarantees Kennedy proposed in his June 1963 speech became federal law, when President Johnson signed the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002492_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002492-207">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_policy">Space policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Space policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Space policy of the United States">Space policy of the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg/210px-Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg/315px-Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg/420px-Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2940" data-file-height="2230" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_Giving_Historic_Speech_to_Congress_-_GPN-2000-001658.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy proposing a program to land men on the Moon to Congress in May 1961. Johnson and Sam Rayburn are seated behind him.</div></div></div>
<p>In the aftermath of the Soviet launch of <i><a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a></i>, the first artificial Earth satellite, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> had proposed a manned <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">lunar landing</a> by the early 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006150–151_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006150–151-208">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Funding for the manned program, known as the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>, was far from certain as Eisenhower held an ambivalent attitude on manned spaceflight.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Early in his presidency, Kennedy was poised to dismantle the manned space program, but he postponed any decision out of deference to Johnson, who had been a strong supporter of the space program in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993138_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993138-210">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Along with <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Wiesner" title="Jerome Wiesner">Jerome Wiesner</a>, Johnson was given a major role in overseeing the administration's space policy, and at Johnson's recommendation Kennedy appointed <a href="/wiki/James_E._Webb" title="James E. Webb">James E. Webb</a> to head NASA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006151–152_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006151–152-211">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In April 1961, Soviet cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> became the first person to fly in space, reinforcing American fears about being left behind in a technological competition with the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek2003393_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek2003393-212">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Less than a month later, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> became the first American to travel into space, strengthening Kennedy's confidence in NASA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006152–153_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006152–153-213">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the aftermath of the Gagarin's flight, as well as the failed Bay of Pigs invasion, Kennedy felt pressured to respond to the perceived erosion of American prestige. He asked Johnson to explore the feasibility of beating the Soviets to the Moon. Though he was concerned about the program's costs, Kennedy agreed to Johnson's recommendation that the U.S. commit to a manned lunar landing as the major objective of the U.S. space program. In a May 25 speech, Kennedy declared,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006152–153_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006152–153-213">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... I believe that this nation should commit itself to achieving the goal, before this decade is out, of landing a man on the Moon and returning him safely to the Earth. No single space project in this period will be more impressive to mankind, or more important for the long-range exploration of space; and none will be so difficult or expensive to accomplish.<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy_at_Congress_1961_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy_at_Congress_1961-214">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Special_Message_to_the_Congress_on_Urgent_National_Needs" class="extiw" title="s:Special Message to the Congress on Urgent National Needs">Full text</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource has information on &quot;Special Message to the Congress on Urgent National Needs&quot;"><img alt="Wikisource has information on &quot;Special Message to the Congress on Urgent National Needs&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/11px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="12" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/17px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/22px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></a>
</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_5" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:322px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_6&quot; <p>Though Gallup polling showed that many in the public were skeptical of the necessity of the Apollo Program, members of Congress were strongly supportive in 1961, and they approved a major increase in NASA's funding. In 1962, <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> became the first American to orbit the Earth, and the following year <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner program</a> sent an unmanned flight past <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>. Though some members of Congress came to favor shifting NASA's budget to other programs, Kennedy and Johnson remained committed to the lunar landing. On July 20, 1969, two American astronauts landed on the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006153–155_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006153–155-215">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_issues">Other issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Other issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_women">Status of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Status of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the 1960 presidential campaign, Kennedy endorsed the concept of <a href="/wiki/Equal_pay_for_equal_work" title="Equal pay for equal work">equal pay for equal work</a>, as well as the adoption of an <a href="/wiki/Equal_Rights_Amendment" title="Equal Rights Amendment">Equal Rights Amendment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006142_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006142-216">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> His key appointee on women's issues was <a href="/wiki/Esther_Peterson" title="Esther Peterson">Esther Peterson</a>, the Director of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Women%27s_Bureau" title="United States Women&#39;s Bureau">United States Women's Bureau</a>, who focused on improving the economic status of women.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006143_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006143-217">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> In December 1961, Kennedy signed an executive order creating the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Commission_on_the_Status_of_Women" title="Presidential Commission on the Status of Women">Presidential Commission on the Status of Women</a> to advise him on issues concerning the status of women.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Former First Lady <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a> led the commission until her death in 1962. The commission's final report, entitled "American Women", was issued in October 1963. The report documented the legal and cultural discrimination women in America faced and made several policy recommendations to bring about change.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1993433_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1993433-219">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The creation of this commission, as well its prominent public profile, prompted Congress to begin considering various bills related to women's status. Among them was the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Pay_Act_of_1963" title="Equal Pay Act of 1963">Equal Pay Act of 1963</a>, an amendment to the <a href="/wiki/Fair_Labor_Standards_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Fair Labor Standards Act">Fair Labor Standards Act</a>, aimed at abolishing wage disparity based on sex; Kennedy signed it into law on June 10, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy also signed an executive order banning sex discrimination in the federal workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996462_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996462-221">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Organized_crime">Organized crime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Organized crime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The issue of <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">organized crime</a> had gained national attention during the 1950s due in part to the investigations of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Select_Committee_on_Improper_Activities_in_Labor_and_Management" title="United States Senate Select Committee on Improper Activities in Labor and Management">McClellan Committee</a>. Both Robert Kennedy and John F. Kennedy had played a role on that committee, and in 1960 Robert Kennedy published the book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Enemy_Within_(Kennedy_book)" title="The Enemy Within (Kennedy book)">The Enemy Within</a></i>, which focused on the influence of organized crime within businesses and organized labor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006145–146_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006145–146-222">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Under the leadership of the attorney general, the Kennedy administration shifted the focus of the Justice Department, the FBI, and the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a> to organized crime. Kennedy also won congressional approval for five bills designed to crack down on interstate <a href="/wiki/Racket_(crime)" title="Racket (crime)">racketeering</a>, gambling, and the transportation of firearms. The federal government targeted prominent <a href="/wiki/American_Mafia" title="American Mafia">Mafia</a> leaders like <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Marcello" title="Carlos Marcello">Carlos Marcello</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joey_Aiuppa" title="Joey Aiuppa">Joey Aiuppa</a>; Marcello was deported to <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, while Aiuppa was convicted of violating of the <a href="/wiki/Migratory_Bird_Treaty_Act_of_1918" title="Migratory Bird Treaty Act of 1918">Migratory Bird Treaty Act of 1918</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006146–148_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006146–148-223">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The attorney general's top target was perhaps <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Hoffa" title="Jimmy Hoffa">Jimmy Hoffa</a>, the head of the <a href="/wiki/International_Brotherhood_of_Teamsters" title="International Brotherhood of Teamsters">Teamsters Union</a>. The Justice Department's "Get Hoffa Squad" ultimately secured the conviction of over 100 Teamsters, including Hoffa, who was convicted of <a href="/wiki/Jury_tampering" title="Jury tampering">jury tampering</a> and pension fund fraud.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006148–149_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006148–149-224">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_and_military_death_penalty">Federal and military death penalty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Federal and military death penalty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As president, Kennedy oversaw the last federal execution prior to <a href="/wiki/Furman_v._Georgia" title="Furman v. Georgia">Furman v. Georgia</a>, a 1972 case that led to a moratorium on federal executions.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Victor_Feguer" title="Victor Feguer">Victor Feguer</a> was sentenced to death by a federal court in Iowa and was executed on March 15, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldberg_2001_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldberg_2001-226">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy commuted a death sentence imposed by a military court on seaman Jimmie Henderson on February 12, 1962, changing the penalty to life in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-Riechmann_2008_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riechmann_2008-227">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> On March 22, 1962, Kennedy signed into law HR5143 (PL87-423), abolishing the mandatory death penalty for <a href="/wiki/First_degree_murder_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="First degree murder in the United States">first degree murder</a> in the District of Columbia, the only remaining jurisdiction in the United States with such a penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-JFKlibrary.org_leg_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFKlibrary.org_leg-228">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_American_relations">Native American relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Native American relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Kinzua_Dam#Native_Americans" title="Kinzua Dam">Kinzua Dam §&#160;Native Americans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seneca_nation#Kinzua_Dam" class="mw-redirect" title="Seneca nation">Seneca nation §&#160;Kinzua Dam</a></div>
<p>Construction of the <a href="/wiki/Kinzua_Dam" title="Kinzua Dam">Kinzua Dam</a> flooded 10,000 acres (4,047&#160;ha) of <a href="/wiki/Seneca_nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Seneca nation">Seneca nation</a> land that they had occupied under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Canandaigua" title="Treaty of Canandaigua">Treaty of 1794</a>, and forced 600 Seneca to relocate to <a href="/wiki/Salamanca_(town),_New_York" title="Salamanca (town), New York">Salamanca, New York</a>. Kennedy was asked by the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_Liberties_Union" title="American Civil Liberties Union">American Civil Liberties Union</a> to intervene and to halt the project, but he declined, citing a critical need for flood control. He expressed concern about the plight of the Seneca, and directed government agencies to assist in obtaining more land, damages, and assistance to help mitigate their displacement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBilharz200255_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBilharz200255-229">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy_letter_1961_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy_letter_1961-230">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Kennedy had relatively little interest in agricultural issues, but he sought to remedy the issue of overproduction, boost the income of farmers, and lower federal expenditures on agriculture. Under the direction of Secretary of Agriculture <a href="/wiki/Orville_Freeman" title="Orville Freeman">Orville Freeman</a>, the administration sought to limit the production of farmers, but these proposals were generally defeated in Congress. To increase demand for domestic agricultural products and help the impoverished, Kennedy launched a pilot <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Nutrition_Assistance_Program" title="Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program">Food Stamp program</a> and expanded the <a href="/wiki/National_School_Lunch_Act" title="National School Lunch Act">federal school lunch program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006109–118_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006109–118-231">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination">Assassination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Assassination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">Assassination of John F. Kennedy</a></div>
<p>President Kennedy was assassinated in <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, at 12:30&#160;pm <a href="/wiki/Central_Time_Zone_(Americas)" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Time Zone (Americas)">Central Standard Time</a> on November 22, 1963, while on a political trip to Texas to smooth over frictions in the Democratic Party between liberals <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Yarborough" title="Ralph Yarborough">Ralph Yarborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Don_Yarborough" title="Don Yarborough">Don Yarborough</a> and conservative <a href="/wiki/John_Connally" title="John Connally">John Connally</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Traveling in a presidential <a href="/wiki/Motorcade" title="Motorcade">motorcade</a> through downtown Dallas with Jackie Kennedy, Connally, and Connally's wife, Nelly, Kennedy was shot in the head and neck. He was taken to <a href="/wiki/Parkland_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="Parkland Hospital">Parkland Hospital</a> for emergency medical treatment, but was pronounced dead at 1:00&#160;pm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996518_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996518-233">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_motorcade,_Dallas_crop.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png/210px-John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png" decoding="async" width="210" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png/315px-John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png/420px-John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png 2x" data-file-width="3641" data-file-height="2444" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_motorcade,_Dallas_crop.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Kennedys and the Connallys in the presidential limousine moments before the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">assassination</a> in Dallas</div></div></div>
<p>Hours after the assassination, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a>, an order filler at the <a href="/wiki/Texas_School_Book_Depository" title="Texas School Book Depository">Texas School Book Depository</a>, was arrested for the murder of police officer <a href="/wiki/J._D._Tippit" title="J. D. Tippit">J. D. Tippit</a>, and was subsequently charged with Kennedy's assassination. Oswald denied the charges, but was killed by strip-club owner <a href="/wiki/Jack_Ruby" title="Jack Ruby">Jack Ruby</a> on November 24. Ruby claimed to have killed Oswald due to his own grief over Kennedy's death, but the assassination of Kennedy and the death of Oswald gave rise to <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories" title="John F. Kennedy assassination conspiracy theories">enormous speculation that Kennedy had been the victim of a conspiracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996518–519_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996518–519-234">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was succeeded as president by Lyndon Johnson, who stated on November 27 that "no memorial or oration or eulogy could more eloquently honor President Kennedy's memory than the earliest possible passage of a civil rights bill for which he fought so long."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996524–525_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996524–525-235">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President Johnson created the <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a>—chaired by Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>—to investigate the assassination. The Warren Commission concluded that Oswald acted alone in killing Kennedy, and that Oswald was not part of any conspiracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002497_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002497-236">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> The results of this investigation are disputed by many.<sup id="cite_ref-Russo_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russo-237">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> Various theories place the blame for the assassination on Cuba, the Soviet Union, the Mafia, the CIA, the FBI, top military leaders, or Lyndon Johnson.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996520–521_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996520–521-238">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> A 2004 Fox News poll found that 66% of Americans thought there had been a conspiracy to kill President Kennedy, while 74% thought that there had been a cover-up.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)#Gallup_Poll" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup Poll</a> in mid-November 2013, showed 61% believed in a conspiracy, and only 30% thought that Oswald did it alone.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> In 1979, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations" title="United States House Select Committee on Assassinations">United States House Select Committee on Assassinations</a> concluded that Oswald shot Kennedy, and that neither a foreign government nor a U.S. governmental institution had been involved in the shooting. However, the committee also found that there was a "high probability" that a second shooter, possibly with connections to the Mafia, had fired at Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996521_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996521-241">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The assassination had an enormous impact on the American public and contributed to a growing distrust of governmental institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996521–522_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson1996521–522-242">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Giglio writes that Kennedy's assassination "invoked immeasurable grief," adding, "[t]o many Americans, John Kennedy's death ended an age of excellence, innocence, hope, and optimism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006303_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006303-243">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, historian Carl M. Brauer concluded that the public's "fascination with the assassination may indicate a psychological denial of Kennedy's death, a mass wish...to undo it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002497_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrauer2002497-236">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_reputation">Historical reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Historical reputation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg/210px-John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg/315px-John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg/420px-John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="1307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F_Kennedy_eternal_flame_after_2013_upgrade_-_2013-05-30.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Eternal_Flame" title="John F. Kennedy Eternal Flame">Eternal Flame</a> at the Kennedy grave site, <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a></div></div></div>
<p>Assassinated in the prime of life, Kennedy remains a powerful and popular symbol of both inspiration and tragedy.<sup id="cite_ref-tgillman_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tgillman-244">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Camelot" title="Camelot">Camelot</a>" is often used to describe his presidency, reflecting both the mythic grandeur accorded Kennedy in death, and the powerful nostalgia that many feel for that era of American history.<sup id="cite_ref-abrinkley1_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abrinkley1-245">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> He is idolized like Abraham Lincoln and Franklin D. Roosevelt; Gallup Poll surveys consistently show his public approval rating to be around 80 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-tgillman_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tgillman-244">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's legacy strongly influenced a generation of <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberal</a> Democratic leaders, including <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dukakis" title="Michael Dukakis">Michael Dukakis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Hart" title="Gary Hart">Gary Hart</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006304_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006304-246">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians and political scientists tend to <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States#Scholar_survey_results" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">rank</a> Kennedy as an above-average president, and he is usually the highest-ranking president who served less than one full term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006308–309_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006308–309-247">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Assessments of his policies are mixed. The early part of his administration carried missteps highlighted by the failed Bay of Pigs invasion and the 1961 Vienna summit.<sup id="cite_ref-JFK-IL_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFK-IL-248">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abrinkley1_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abrinkley1-245">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> The second half of his presidency was filled with several notable successes, for which he receives acclaim. He skillfully handled the Cuban Missile Crisis, as he avoided nuclear war and set the stage for a less tense era of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Soviet Union–United States relations">U.S.–Soviet</a> relations.<sup id="cite_ref-JFK-IL_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFK-IL-248">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abrinkley1_245-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abrinkley1-245">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, his escalation of the U.S. presence in Vietnam has been criticized.<sup id="cite_ref-JFK-IL_248-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFK-IL-248">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's effectiveness in domestic affairs has also been the subject of debate. Giglio notes that many of Kennedy's proposals were adopted by Congress, but his most important programs, including health insurance for the elderly, federal aid to education, and tax reform, were blocked during his presidency.
<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006122–123_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiglio2006122–123-249">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Many of Kennedy's proposals were passed after his death, during the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Presidency of Lyndon B. Johnson">Johnson administration</a>, and Kennedy's death gave those proposals a powerful moral component.<sup id="cite_ref-tgillman_244-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tgillman-244">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> survey of 162 members of the <a href="/wiki/American_Political_Science_Association" title="American Political Science Association">American Political Science Association</a>'s Presidents and Executive Politics section ranked Kennedy 14th highest overall among the 43 persons who have been president, including then-president Barack Obama. Then among the "modern presidents", the thirteen from Franklin Roosevelt through Obama, he places in the middle of the pack. The survey also found Kennedy to be the most overrated U.S. president.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> A 2017 <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a> survey has Kennedy ranked among the top ten presidents of all-time. The survey asked 91 presidential historians to rank the 43 former presidents (including then-president Barack Obama) in various categories to come up with a composite score, resulting in an overall ranking. Kennedy was ranked 8th among all former presidents (down from 6th in 2009). His rankings in the various categories of this most recent poll were as follows: public persuasion (6), crisis leadership (7), economic management (7), moral authority (15), international relations (14), administrative skills (15), relations with congress (12), vision/setting an agenda (9), pursued equal justice for all (7), performance with context of times (9).<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> A 2018 poll of the <a href="/wiki/American_Political_Science_Association" title="American Political Science Association">American Political Science Association</a>’s Presidents and Executive Politics section ranked Kennedy as the 16th best president.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> A 2006 poll of historians ranked Kennedy's decision to authorize the Bay of Pigs invasion as the eighth-worst mistake made by a sitting president.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Unpledged-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Unpledged_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Southern_Democrats" title="Southern Democrats">Southern Democrats</a> in several states who were opposed to the national Democratic Party's support for <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">voting rights</a> for <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African Americans</a> living in the South, attempted to block Kennedy's election by denying him the necessary number of electoral votes (269 of 537) for victory.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDudleyShiraev200883_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDudleyShiraev200883-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Faithless-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Faithless_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Henry_D._Irwin" title="Henry D. Irwin">Henry D. Irwin</a>, who had been pledged to vote for Nixon.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Roosevelt-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Roosevelt_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> was nine months younger when he first assumed the presidency on September 14, 1901, but he was not elected to the office until 1904, when he was 46.<sup id="cite_ref-companionJFK_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-companionJFK-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-191"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-191">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The groups were: American Jewish Congress, American Veterans Committee, Americans for Democratic Action, Anti-Defamation League of B'Nai B'rith, International Union of Electrical Workers (AFL-CIO), National Assn. for the Advancement of Colored People, and United Automobile Workers (AFL-CIO).</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
